There were 601 pairs breeding in Armenia and around 700 pairs in the Netherlands in 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-Vogelbescherming_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vogelbescherming-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> and few pairs also breed in South Africa, typically recent colonists from within the normal wintering population.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> In Poland, electric poles have been modified with a platform at the top to prevent the White Stork's large nest from disrupting the electricity supply, and sometimes nests are moved from an electric pole to a man-made platform.<sup id="cite_ref-AZ-2009_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AZ-2009-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Introductions of zoo-reared birds in the Netherlands has been followed up by feeding and nest-building programs by volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-Vogelbescherming_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vogelbescherming-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Similar reintroduction programs are taking place in Sweden,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> and Switzerland,<sup id="cite_ref-Moritzi_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moritzi-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> where 175 pairs were recorded breeding in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Long term viability of the population in Switzerland is unclear as breeding success rates are low, and supplementary feeding does not appear to be of benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-Moritzi_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moritzi-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=White_Stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cultural associations">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_associations">Cultural associations</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg/220px-ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg/330px-ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg/440px-ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChelmonskiJozef.1900.Bociany.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
"Bociany" (Storks), a painting by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski" title="Józef Chełmoński" class="mw-redirect">Józef Chełmoński</a> (1849–1914)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to its large size, predation on vermin, and nesting behaviour close to human settlements and on rooftops, the White Stork has an imposing presence that has had an impact on human culture and folklore.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, it was associated with, and was the <a href="/wiki/Hieroglyph" title="Hieroglyph">hieroglyph</a> for, the <i><a href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul#Ba_.28soul.29" title="Egyptian soul" class="mw-redirect">Ba</a></i>, or "soul".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew" class="mw-redirect">Hebrew</a> word for the White Stork is <i>chasidah</i> (חסידה), meaning "merciful" or "kind".<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a> portray storks as models of parental devotion, and it was believed that they did not die of old age, but flew to islands and took the appearance of humans. The bird is featured in two of <a href="/wiki/Aesop%27s_Fables" title="Aesop's Fables">Aesop's Fables</a>: <a href="/wiki/The_Fox_and_the_Stork" title="The Fox and the Stork">The Fox and the Stork</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Farmer_and_the_Stork" title="The Farmer and the Stork">The Farmer and the Stork</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> They were also thought to care for their aged parents, feeding them and even transporting them, and children's books depicted them as a model of filial values. A Greek law called <i>Pelargonia</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> word <i>pelargos</i> for stork, required citizens to take care of their aged parents.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> The Greeks also held that killing a stork could be punished with death.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> It was allegedly protected in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Thessaly" title="Ancient Thessaly">Ancient Thessaly</a> as it hunted snakes, and widely held to be <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>'s "white bird".<sup id="cite_ref-Royds14_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Royds14-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Roman writers noted the White Stork's arrival in spring, which alerted farmers to plant their vines.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Followers of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> revered storks because they made an annual pilgrimage to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> on their migration.<sup id="cite_ref-sax_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sax-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Some of the earliest understanding on bird migration were initiated by an interest in White Storks; <i><a href="/wiki/Pfeilstorch" title="Pfeilstorch">Pfeilstorch</a></i> ("arrow storks") were found in Europe with African <a href="/wiki/Arrow" title="Arrow">arrows</a> embedded in their bodies. A well-known example of such a stork found in the summer of 1822 in the German town of <a href="/wiki/Kl%C3%BCtz" title="Klütz">Klütz</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecklenburg" title="Mecklenburg">Mecklenburg</a> was made into a mounted taxidermy specimen, complete with the ornate African arrow, that is now in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rostock" title="University of Rostock">University of Rostock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-arrow_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arrow-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_moral_education.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Stork_moral_education.jpg/170px-Stork_moral_education.jpg" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Stork_moral_education.jpg/255px-Stork_moral_education.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Stork_moral_education.jpg/340px-Stork_moral_education.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_moral_education.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Supposed filial virtues of the stork<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Storks have little fear of humans if not disturbed, and often nest on buildings in Europe. In Germany, the presence of a nest on a house was believed to protect against fires. They were also protected because of the belief that their souls were human.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> German and Dutch households would encourage storks to nest on houses, sometimes by constructing purpose-built high platforms, to bring good luck.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Poles, Lithuanians and Ukrainians believe that storks bring harmony to a family on whose property they nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_of_Ukraine_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_of_Ukraine-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The White Stork is a popular <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motif</a> on <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamps</a>, and it is featured on more than 120 stamps issued by more than 60 stamp-issuing entities.<sup id="cite_ref-Birdtheme_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birdtheme-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_birds" title="List of national birds">national bird</a> of Lithuania,<sup id="cite_ref-Lithuania_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lithuania-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> and it was a Polish mascot at the <a href="/wiki/Expo_2000" title="Expo 2000">Expo 2000</a> Fair in Hanover.<sup id="cite_ref-paiiz_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paiiz-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 19th century, storks were also thought to only live in countries having a republican form of government.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Polish poet <a href="/wiki/Cyprian_Kamil_Norwid" title="Cyprian Kamil Norwid" class="mw-redirect">Cyprian Kamil Norwid</a> mentioned storks in his poem <i>Moja piosnka (II)</i> ("My Song (II)"):<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">
<p>For the land where it's a great travesty<br />
To harm a stork's nest in a pear tree,<br />
For storks serve us all...<br />
I am homesick, Lord!...</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=White_Stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Storks and childbirth">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Storks_and_childbirth">Storks and childbirth</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg/120px-Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg" width="120" height="423" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg/180px-Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg/240px-Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
"Der Klapperstorch" (The Stork), a painting by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Spitzweg" title="Carl Spitzweg">Carl Spitzweg</a> (1808–1885)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to European folklore, the stork is responsible for bringing babies to new parents. The legend is very ancient, but was popularised by a 19th century <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_Andersen" title="Hans Christian Andersen">Hans Christian Andersen</a> story called <i>The Storks</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-sax_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sax-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> German folklore held that storks found babies in caves or marshes and brought them to households in a basket on their backs or held in their beaks. These caves contained <i>adebarsteine</i> or "stork stones". The babies would then be given to the mother or dropped down the chimney. Households would notify when they wanted children by placing sweets for the stork on the window sill.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> From there the folklore has spread around the world to countries such as the Philippines and South America.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Slavic_mythology" title="Slavic mythology">Slavic mythology and religion</a>, storks were thought to carry unborn <a href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)" class="mw-redirect">souls</a> from <a href="/wiki/Iriy" title="Iriy">Iriy</a> to Earth in spring and summer.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> This belief still persists in the modern folk culture of many Slavic countries, in the simplified child story that "storks bring children into the world".<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> Storks were seen by the Slavs as bringing luck, and killing one would bring misfortune.<sup id="cite_ref-autonazwa1_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autonazwa1-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> A long-term study that showed a <a href="/wiki/Spurious_correlation" title="Spurious correlation" class="mw-redirect">spurious correlation</a> between the numbers of stork nests and human births is widely used in the teaching of basic <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> as an example to highlight that <a href="/wiki/Correlation_and_dependence" title="Correlation and dependence">correlation</a> does not necessarily indicate <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> The child-bringing myth has appeared in different forms in history. Children of <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_US" title="Slavery in the US" class="mw-redirect">slaves</a> were sometimes told that white babies were brought by storks, while black babies were born from <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/buzzard" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:buzzard">buzzard</a> eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Psychoanalyst Marvin Margolis suggests the enduring nature of the stork fable of the newborn is linked to its addressing a psychological need, in that it allays the discomfort of discussing sex and procreation with children. Birds have long been associated with the maternal symbols from pagan goddesses such as <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Ghost</a>, and the stork may have been chosen for its white plumage (depicting purity), size (in that it is big enough to carry an infant), and flight at high altitude (likened to flying between Earth and Heaven).<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> The fable and its relation to the internal world of the child have been discussed by <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jung10_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jung10-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> In fact, Jung recalled being told the story himself upon the birth of his own sister.<sup id="cite_ref-Pickles11_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pickles11-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> The traditional link with the newborn continues with their use in <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a> for such products as <a href="/wiki/Diaper" title="Diaper">nappies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baby_announcement" title="Baby announcement">birth announcements</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There were negative aspects to stork folklore as well; a Polish folk tale relates how God made the stork's plumage white, while the Devil gave it black wings, imbuing it with both good and evil impulses. They were also associated with handicapped or stillborn babies in Germany, explained as the stork having dropped the baby <i>en route</i> to the household, or as revenge or punishment for past wrongdoing. A mother who was confined to bed around the time of childbirth was said to have been "bitten" by the stork.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> In Denmark, storks were said to toss a nestling off the nest and then an egg in successive years.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_122-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> In medieval England, storks were also associated with adultery, possibly inspired by their courtship rituals. Their preening and posture saw them linked with the attribute of self-conceit.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=White_Stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Note">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Note">Note</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By convention, length is measured from the tip of the bill to the tip of the tail on a dead bird (or skin) laid on its back.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=White_Stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: References">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
